We discover 40 new galaxy-scale strong lenses, which we supplement with 58 previously discovered SLACS lenses. In addition, we determine the posterior PDFs of the Einstein radius for 33 galaxies (18 new and 15 from legacy SLACS data) based on single lensed images. We find a less-than-unity slope of 0.64 \u00b1 0.06 for the {{log }<SUB>10</SUB>}{{\u03c3 }<SUB>*</SUB>}-{{log }<SUB>10</SUB>}{{\u03c3 }<SUB>SIE</SUB>} relation, which corresponds to a 6\u03c3 evidence that the total mass-density profile of early-type galaxies varies systematically in the sense of being shallower at higher lens-galaxy velocity dispersions. The trend is only significant when single-image systems are considered, highlighting the importance of including both \u201clenses\u201d and \u201cnonlenses\u201d for an unbiased treatment of the lens population when extending to lower mass ranges. By scaling simple stellar-population models to the HST I-band data, we identify a strong trend of increasing dark-matter fraction at higher velocity dispersions, which can be alternatively interpreted as a trend in the stellar initial mass function (IMF) normalization. Consistent with previous findings and the suggestion of a nonuniversal IMF, we find that a Salpeter IMF is ruled out for galaxies with velocity dispersion less than 180 km s<SUP>-1</SUP>. Considered together, our mass-profile and dark-matter fraction trends with increasing galaxy mass could both be explained by an increasing relative contribution on kiloparsec scales from a dark-matter halo with a spatial profile more extended than that of the stellar component. <P />Based on observations made with the NASA/ESA Hubble Space Telescope, obtained at the Space Telescope Science Institute, which is operated by AURA, Inc., under NASA Contract NAS 5-26555. These observations are associated with Programs #10174, #10494, #10587, #10798, #10886, and #12210.", "doi": "10.1088/0004-637X/803/2/71", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:1407.2240", "issn": "0004-637X", <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">THE SLOAN LENS ACS SURVEY. All rights reserved.</span><br />\n    <!-- end Copyright -->\n<span itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Periodical\" itemid=\"periodical\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-breadcrumb-title article-toolbar-anchor\">\n\t<span itemprop=\"name\">\n\t\t<a href=\"/journal/0004-637X\" itemprop=\"url\">The Astrophysical Journal</a></span></span>,\n\t<span itemprop=\"isPartOf\" itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/PublicationVolume\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-breadcrumb-vol nowrap\">\n\t<link itemprop=\"isPartOf\" itemid=\"periodical\" itemscope/>\n\t<span itemprop=\"volumeNumber\"><a href=\"/volume/0004-637X/803\" itemprop=\"url\">Volume 803</a></span></span>,\n<span itemprop=\"issueNumber\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-breadcrumb-issue\"><a href=\"/issue/0004-637X/803/2\" itemprop=\"url\"><!--Issue Number Tile Start-->\nNumber 2<!--Issue Number Tile End--></a></span>\n<!--Article Breadcrumb Tile End-->\n<!-- Start Focus issue title -->\n\t<!-- End focus issue title -->\n\t<span style=\"display: block\"><b>Citation</b> Yiping Shu <em>et al</em> 2015 <em>ApJ</em> <b>803</b> 71</span>\n    </p>\n\n<div class=\"btn-multi-block mb-1\">\n\t\t<a href=\"/article/10.1088/0004-637X/803/2/71/pdf\" class=\"btn btn-large btn-primary content-download btn-full-w-mobile wd-jnl-art-pdf-button-main dupe-buttons\" title=\"THE SLOAN LENS ACS SURVEY. <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F803%2F2%2F71\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/803/2/71</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <P>We present observational results from a new <I>Hubble Space Telescope</I> (<I>HST</I>) Snapshot program to extend the methods of the Sloan Lens ACS (SLACS) Survey to lower lens-galaxy masses. We discover 40 new galaxy-scale strong lenses, which we supplement with 58 previously discovered SLACS lenses. In addition, we determine the posterior PDFs of the Einstein radius for 33 galaxies (18 new and 15 from legacy SLACS data) based on single lensed images. We find a less-than-unity slope of 0.64 \u00b1 0.06 for the <IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/apj509959ieqn1.gif\"></IMG>\u2212<IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/apj509959ieqn2.gif\"></IMG> relation, which corresponds to a 6<I>\u03c3</I> evidence that the total mass\u2013density profile of early-type galaxies varies systematically in the sense of being shallower at higher lens-galaxy velocity dispersions. The trend is only significant when single-image systems are considered, highlighting the importance of including both \"lenses\" and \"nonlenses\" for an unbiased treatment of the lens population when extending to lower mass ranges. By scaling simple stellar-population models to the <I>HST</I> I-band data, we identify a strong trend of increasing dark-matter fraction at higher velocity dispersions, which can be alternatively interpreted as a trend in the stellar initial mass function (IMF) normalization. Consistent with previous findings and the suggestion of a nonuniversal IMF, we find that a Salpeter IMF is ruled out for galaxies with velocity dispersion less than 180 km s<SUP>\u22121</SUP>. Considered together, our mass-profile and dark-matter fraction trends with increasing galaxy mass could both be explained by an increasing relative contribution on kiloparsec scales from a dark-matter halo with a spatial profile more extended than that of the stellar component.</P></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj509959s1\" name=\"apj509959s1\">1.\u00a0INTRODUCTION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Early-type galaxies (ETGs), classified by their morphology, compose one of the two main categories of galaxies (Hubble <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib63\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib63\">1926</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib64\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib64\">1936</a>). Although considered to be relatively <em>\"dead\"</em> and <em>\"featureless\"</em> as a consequence of their little star-formation activities and smooth light distributions, ETGs play a crucial role in studying the evolution of galaxies, the nature of dark matter, and cosmology. Believed to be the end products of a hierarchical merging scenario (Toomre &amp; Toomre <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib112\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib112\">1972</a>; White &amp; Frenk <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib127\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib127\">1991</a>; Kauffmann et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib68\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib68\">1993</a>; Cole et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib28\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib28\">2000</a>), their structures, properties, and formation histories can be used as a compelling test of the \u039b Cold Dark Matter (\u039bCDM) paradigm. Additionally, ETGs can be extremely luminous and therefore can be used as powerful cosmological tracers of the large-scale structure (Eisenstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib39\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib39\">2005</a>; Percival et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib97\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib97\">2007</a>; Anderson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib3\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib3\">2012</a>).</p><p>However, the formation and evolution of ETGs are still puzzling, and further investigations are highly demanded. Concerning the mass\u2013density profile of ETGs, N-body DM-only numerical simulations have revealed a somewhat \"universal\" density profile with a <em>r</em><sup>\u22121</sup> inner profile and a <em>r</em><sup>\u22123</sup> drop-off at large radii, independent of the halo mass (Navarro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib87\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib87\">1996</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib88\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib88\">1997</a>). Later on, various observations of DM-dominated galaxies yield inconsistent inner-density slopes with numerical simulations (Moore et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib84\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib84\">1999</a>; Graham et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib59\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib59\">2006</a>; Navarro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib89\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib89\">2010</a>), the tension of which can be loosened by taking baryonic physics into account. Gas cooling permits baryons to condense in the central regions of galaxies, and therefore it is believed to make the mass distribution more centrally concentrated (e.g., Gnedin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib54\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib54\">2004</a>; Gustafsson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib61\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib61\">2006</a>; Abadi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib1\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib1\">2010</a>; Velliscig et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib126\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib126\">2014</a>). Heating due to dynamical friction and supernovae (SN)/active galactic nucleus (AGN) feedback, in contrast, can soften the central density concentration (e.g., Nipoti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib94\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib94\">2004</a>; Romano-D\u00edaz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib99\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib99\">2008</a>; Duffy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib35\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib35\">2010</a>; Governato et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib57\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib57\">2010</a>; Martizzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib81\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib81\">2012</a>; Dubois et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib34\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib34\">2013</a>; Velliscig et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib126\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib126\">2014</a>). The strength of these competing effects differs from galaxy to galaxy, and hence studying the dependences of the shape of the mass\u2013density profile in the central region on galaxy mass, redshift, and other structural quantities unravels the formation and evolution of ETGs.</p><p>The stellar initial mass function (IMF) is an empirical relation quantifying the relative fraction of stars as a function of the stellar mass at the time when the whole population formed. Salpeter (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib100\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib100\">1955</a>) first quantified the IMF as a simple power-law function, using main-sequence stars in the solar neighborhood. Later on, various modifications have been considered at the low-mass end, and the most commonly used forms are the Kroupa IMF (Kroupa <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib78\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib78\">2001</a>) and the Chabrier IMF (Chabrier <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib26\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib26\">2003</a>). The IMF of a galaxy could depend on the environmental properties of the molecular cloud it originated from, such as metallicity, temperature, and density, and could therefore be nonuniversal. Having knowledge of the form and the variation of the IMF provides deep insights in understanding the role of the environment during star formation and galaxy evolution processes. Recently, several pieces of evidence suggest that the IMF indeed varies (e.g., Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib7\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib7\">2010b</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib116\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib116\">2010</a>; van Dokkum &amp; Conroy <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib123\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib123\">2010</a>; Strader et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib110\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib110\">2011</a>; Cappellari et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib24\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib24\">2012</a>; Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib104\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib104\">2012</a>; Spiniello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib109\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib109\">2012</a>; Conroy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib29\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib29\">2013</a>; Ferreras et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib43\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib43\">2013</a>; La Barbera et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib79\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib79\">2013</a>; Tortora et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib114\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib114\">2013</a>; Brewer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib19\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib19\">2014</a>; Spiniello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib108\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib108\">2014</a>).</p><p>Strong gravitational lensing (GL) has its unique power among the many techniques for the study of ETGs. As a pure gravity-dependent effect, GL provides highly accurate measurements of total mass that are robust against different models and assumptions about galaxy properties. Therefore, it provides the best estimation of the total projected mass within the so-called <em>Einstein radius</em> enclosed by the lensed images of the background object.</p><p>One of the known issues in estimating the enclosed mass by GL is the mass-sheet degeneracy (MSD), in which a proper mass\u2013density transformation preserves the dimensionless lensing observables, such as image positions, shapes, flux ratios, etc. (e.g., Falco et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib42\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib42\">1985</a>; Gorenstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib56\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib56\">1988</a>; Narayan &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib86\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib86\">1996</a>; Schneider &amp; Sluse <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib102\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib102\">2013</a>). Given lensing data alone, breaking this degeneracy requires some additional physical assumptions. However, following Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib117\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib117\">2009</a>), qualitative estimations of the effect of MSD on our final results are presented in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj509959s4\">4</a>. Further information, including stellar kinematics, can fully break the MSD degeneracy.</p><p>Various lensing surveys have been conducted in the past decade and led to numerous important results. The Lenses Structure and Dynamics Survey aimed to measure the stellar kinematics of a small sample of E/S0 galaxy lenses and combine it with GL to constrain the central mass distribution (Koopmans &amp; Treu <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib73\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib73\">2002</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib74\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib74\">2003</a>; Treu &amp; Koopmans <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib119\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib119\">2002</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib120\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib120\">2004</a>). The SLACS survey (Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib14\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib14\">2006</a>; Koopmans et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib75\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib75\">2006</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib118\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib118\">2006</a>; Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib51\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib51\">2007</a>; Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib13\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib13\">2008a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib15\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib15\">2008b</a>; Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib49\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib49\">2008</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib117\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib117\">2009</a>; Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib5\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib5\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib6\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib6\">2010a</a>; Newton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib93\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib93\">2011</a>) is by far the most productive survey for galaxy-scale strong lenses with known lens and source redshifts, with a discovery of over 90 spectroscopically selected lenses confirmed by high-resolution <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) follow-up. SLACS observes relatively low-redshift (<em>z</em><sub><em>L</em></sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0.4) ETG lens candidates selected from the Luminous Red Galaxy (Eisenstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib38\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib38\">2001</a>) and MAIN (Strauss et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib111\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib111\">2002</a>) galaxy samples of the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS, York et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib129\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib129\">2000</a>). The SLACS survey has yielded multiple novel results on the structure and dynamics of ETGs, which are detailed in the previous papers of this series. Recently, the technique of spectroscopic lens selection has been extended to earlier cosmic time (higher redshift) by the BOSS Emission-Line Lens Survey (BELLS, Brownstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib21\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib21\">2012</a>), which has confirmed 25 strong lenses (0.4 <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> <em>z</em><sub><em>L</em></sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0.7), using data from the Baryon Oscillation Spectroscopic Survey (Dawson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib33\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib33\">2013</a>) of the SDSS-III (Eisenstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib40\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib40\">2011</a>).</p><p>The SLACS sample is a unique resource for studies of the structure of ETGs. As studied by Arneson et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib4\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib4\">2012</a>), no significant bias in the mass axis ratio relative to the parent population is detected in spectroscopically selected lens samples (see also Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib106\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib106\">2014</a>). Additionally,\u00a0the selection bias in the mass\u2013density profile slope is rather small, estimated by Bolton et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib17\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib17\">2012b</a>) to be on the order of ~0.01 for a combined sample of SLACS and BELLS lenses. However, it is known that the SLACS selection function favors the high-mass end due to several related factors. First, strong-lensing cross section (an approximation of the lensing possibility in general) increases with the lens-galaxy mass, so high-mass ETGs are more likely to act as strong lenses. Second, even if a low-mass galaxy acts as a strong lens, the characteristic angular separation of the lensed images will be small and hard to resolve even at space-based imaging resolution. Third, low-mass galaxies can be intrinsically too faint to be selected for SDSS spectroscopy. Fourth, for the preceding reasons, high-mass SLACS candidates have been prioritized for <em>HST</em> follow-up during previous SLACS programs, in order to maximize the survey success rate.</p><p>In order to extend the power of strong lensing to low-mass galaxies, an extension of the SLACS survey known as \"SLACS for the Masses\" (hereafter S4TM, <em>HST</em> Snapshot Program 12210) was initiated in 2012 April with a focus on lens candidates with lower masses and smaller predicted Einstein radii, as compared to SLACS lenses. While the lensing confirmation rate of S4TM is lower than that of previous SLACS <em>HST</em> programs, it importantly achieves a wider lens-mass baseline in combination with previous SLACS lenses. Note that the Sloan WFC Edge-on Late-type Lens Survey (SWELLS, Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib115\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib115\">2011</a>) also probes low-mass lenses, albeit with a focus on spiral galaxies. We refer the readers to the S4TM catalog paper by Brownstein et al. (2015, in preparation) for a full description of the S4TM program details. In this paper, we present the first scientific results on the total mass\u2013density profile and dark-matter content of an extended ETG sample combining the S4TM lenses and previous SLACS lenses. We use a hierarchical Bayesian method to infer the mass-profile scaling relation of the combined lens sample\u00a0and estimate stellar masses through single stellar-population (SSP) model scalings to the observed <em>HST</em> photometry.</p><p>This paper is organized as follows. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj509959s2\">2</a>, we briefly describe our lens identification technique using the SDSS spectroscopy and high-resolution imaging data observed by the <em>HST</em>. Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj509959s3\">3</a> describes our parametric lens-modeling method. We then derive the main findings in Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj509959s4\">4</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj509959s5\">5</a> with regard\u00a0to the study of the mass\u2013density profile and dark-matter fraction of the ETGs. Discussion and conclusions are presented in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj509959s6\">6</a>. Throughout the paper, we assume a standard cosmology with \u03a9<sub><em>m</em></sub> = 0.274, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub> = 0.726, and <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> = 70 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup> (WMAP7, Komatsu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib72\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib72\">2011</a>).</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj509959s2\" name=\"apj509959s2\">2.\u00a0LENS CANDIDATE IDENTIFICATION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The S4TM survey is a snapshot program designed to extend strong GL observations toward lower masses and relatively smaller Einstein radii, as compared to previous SLACS programs. Using the same lens-searching technique as SLACS, 137 lens candidates were identified from the seventh data release of the SDSS (Abazajian et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib2\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib2\">2009</a>) and awarded as <em>HST</em> snapshot targets in Observing Cycle 18. The details about the lens selection technique can be found in the papers by Bolton et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib14\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib14\">2006</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib13\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib13\">2008a</a>) and Brownstein et al. 2015 (in preparation). The basic approach is to search for high-redshift emission lines such as [O<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] doublets, H<em>\u03b2</em>, and [O<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] superimposed on the spectra of SDSS target galaxies at lower redshifts. Such emission lines, associated with star-forming galaxies more distant along the same line of sight, indicate the presence of a candidate lensing system\u00a0and also allow us to simultaneously determine the redshifts of the background objects.</p><p>Between 2010 September and 2012 June, 118 out of 137 candidates were successfully observed with an exposure time of 420 s, each with a single exposure through the F814W filter of the Wide Field Channel (WFC) of the Advanced Camera for Surveys (ACS). The images were visually inspected using the <em>ACSPROC</em> software, a GUI tool implemented by Brownstein et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib21\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib21\">2012</a>). By searching for lensed features in the b-spline-subtracted residual images (Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib14\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib14\">2006</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib13\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib13\">2008a</a>), we have confirmed 40 strong gravitational lenses with clear and definite multiple lensed images\u00a0or even complete Einstein rings (classified as \"grade-A\"), eight systems with strong evidence of multiple imaging but insufficient signal-to-noise ratio (S/N) for definite conclusion and/or modeling (classified as \"grade-B\"), as well as 18 systems showing clear images of the background objects but no clear counterimages (classified as \"grade-C\"). We exclude one grade-C lens (SDSSJ1310 + 0220) from now on as it turned out to be a face-on late-type galaxy with strong emission lines after an examination of its SDSS spectrum.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj509959s3\" name=\"apj509959s3\">3.\u00a0LENS MODELING AND SAMPLE DEFINITION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>For the foreground lens galaxies, we consider a singular isothermal ellipsoid (SIE) lens model (Kassiola &amp; Kovner <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib67\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib67\">1993</a>; Kormann et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib77\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib77\">1994</a>; Keeton &amp; Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib69\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib69\">1998</a>; Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib13\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib13\">2008a</a>) that is generalized from a singular isothermal sphere model, in which the 2D surface mass density falls off as <em>R</em><sup>\u22121</sup>\u00a0but consists of elliptical isodensity contours specified by position angle, P.A., and minor-to-major axis ratio,\u00a0<em>q</em><sub>SIE</sub>. We do not include external shear in our lens model, as it has been shown by Koopmans et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib75\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib75\">2006</a>) and\u00a0Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib117\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib117\">2009</a>)\u00a0and confirmed again using our grade-A lens sample to be a minor effect. Discussions on the effect of external shear on the final results are presented in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj509959s4-3\">4.3</a>. The SIE lens model is characterized by the lensing strength,\u00a0<em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> (specified according to an \"intermediate axis\" convention for elliptical models),</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj509959eqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959eqn1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{{b}_{{\\rm SIE}}}=4\\pi \\frac{\\sigma _{{\\rm SIE}}^{2}}{{{c}^{2}}}\\frac{{{d}_{{\\rm LS}}}}{{{d}_{S}}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>d</em><sub>LS</sub> and <em>d</em><sub><em>S</em></sub> are the angular diameter distances from the lens and the observer to the source, respectively.</p><p>The relationship between the <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> Einstein-radius parameter and the \"lens-model velocity dispersion,\" <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub>,\u00a0given in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj509959eqn1\">1</a>) is, strictly speaking, only valid for the case of circular symmetry. For the elliptical case, we take Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj509959eqn1\">1</a>) to be our implicit definition of <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub>. By this convention, <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> is the intermediate axis (geometric mean of the semimajor and semiminor axes) of the isodensity contour, within which the average convergence,\u00a0<em>\u03ba,</em> is equal to unity. As discussed by Huterer et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib65\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib65\">2005</a>), an alternative convention is to adopt the radius of a circle (denoted <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> in that work), within which the average <em>\u03ba</em> is unity. We adopt the <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> convention in this work to enable us to use the empirical relation of Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib6\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib6\">2010a</a>) (which adopts the same convention) between the mass\u2013density profile and the ratio of stellar to lensing velocity dispersions. We explore the sensitivity of our results to this choice in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj509959s4-3\">4.3</a>\u00a0using the ellipticity-dependent <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub>\u2013<em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> relation from Huterer et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib65\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib65\">2005</a>), which is accurate to &lt;1%, on average, within the axis-ratio ranges of our lenses.</p><p>The surface brightness distributions of the sources are represented by either one or multiple S\u00e9rsic components with the form</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj509959eqn2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959eqn2.gif\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&I\\left( x,y \\right)\\propto {\\rm exp} \\left[ -\\frac{1}{2}{{\\left( \\frac{q{{x}^{2}}+{{y}^{2}}/q}{{{\\sigma }^{2}}} \\right)}^{n/2}} \\right],\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>with the axis ratio,\u00a0<em>q</em>, width,\u00a0<em>\u03c3</em>, and exponent,\u00a0<em>n,</em> as free parameters.</p><p>For a particular SIE lens model and specific composition of the source, one can generate the predicted lensed images via the ray-tracing technique, according to the analytical expressions of the lens equation (Kormann et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib77\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib77\">1994</a>). We apply different fitting strategies to grade-A and grade-C lenses, as explained in the following subsections. Note that for all the lens imaging data, we rescale the corresponding errors, such that the average error of the background matches the standard deviation of the background to correct for possible correlations in the errors caused by image resampling.</p><h3 id=\"apj509959s3-1\" name=\"apj509959s3-1\">3.1.\u00a0Lens Modeling: Grade-A Lenses</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>For each of the 40 newly discovered grade-A lenses in the S4TM survey, the fitting strategy is relatively straightforward. A Levenberg\u2013Marquardt nonlinear least-squares fit (MPFIT, Mor\u00e9 <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib85\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib85\">1978</a>; Markwardt <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib80\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib80\">2009</a>) to the observed lensed images is performed to obtain the best-fit parameters for both the lens and the source. Distinct multiple lensed images/rings ensure accurate and unambiguous model fits. We use the position angle, P.A.<sub>*,</sub> and axis ratio,\u00a0<em>q</em><sub>*,</sub> extracted from the b-spline fit to the light distribution as initial guesses. The starting value for <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> is determined from the separation between a lensed image and its counterimage. Depending on the configuration of the lensing features, one or multiple source components are considered to ensure a reasonably good fit.</p><p>We also apply the same fitting strategy to 58 grade-A lenses found in the SLACS survey\u00a0and show the results in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f1\">1</a>. It is clear that the derived Einstein radii of this work are consistent with the published values in Bolton et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib13\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib13\">2008a</a>), with an average scatter of 0.5%. In subsequent analyses, we adopt this scatter as the fractional uncertainty in the measured Einstein radii for grade-A lenses because the statistical errors significantly underestimate the uncertainties, as discussed by Bolton et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib13\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib13\">2008a</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj509959f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj509959f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Comparison of the derived Einstein radii between this work and the published SLACS values for 58 grade-A lenses confirmed by the SLACS survey. The ratios are consistent with 1, with an rms = 0.005, which suggests that our code is robust.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj509959f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadFigure/figure/apj509959f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj509959f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj509959f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f2\">2</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f3\">3</a> compare the axis ratios and position angles of the light distribution to those of the mass distribution for the grade-A subsample. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f2\">2</a> displays the ratios of the minor-to-major axis ratio as a function of the SDSS stellar velocity dispersion,\u00a0<em>\u03c3</em><sub>SDSS</sub>, which is consistent with 1.0, with an rms scatter of 0.2. No correlation with the velocity dispersion (an approximation of the total mass) is observed. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f3\">3</a> visualizes the difference in the position angle, \u0394P.A. = P.A. \u2212 P.A.<sub>*</sub>, of lenses with respect to the axis ratios. <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn3.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {\\rm \\ \\Delta\\ P}.{\\rm A}{\\rm .}\\rangle =-2{}^\\circ $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {\\rm \\ \\Delta\\ P}.{\\rm A}{\\rm .}\\rangle =-2{}^\\circ</script></span></span>, with an rms spread of 34\u00b0. Clearly, as either <em>q</em><sub>SIE</sub> or <em>q</em><sub>*</sub> goes to 1, the position angle becomes ill-determined, and the scatter increases significantly. Note that <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn4.gif\" alt=\"$|{\\rm \\ \\Delta\\ }|{\\rm P}.{\\rm A}{\\rm .}|$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">|{\\rm \\ \\Delta\\ }|{\\rm P}.{\\rm A}{\\rm .}|</script></span></span> are smaller than 31\u00b0 for all but one S4TM grade-A lens, with both <em>q</em><sub>*</sub> and <em>q</em><sub>SIE</sub> less than 0.8. Therefore, in general, the hypothesis of light tracing mass is valid in terms of both the match of isophotal and isodensity contours and the position-angle alignment and indicates little external perturbing potential. In general, our SIE lens models with multiple parameterized sources can successfully recover the overall lensing features, as well as small details.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj509959f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj509959f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Ratio between the minor-to-major axis ratio determined from SIE model fitting,\u00a0<em>q</em><sub>SIE</sub>, and that measured from light distribution,\u00a0<em>q</em><sub>*</sub>, as a function of velocity dispersion for 40 S4TM grade-A lenses. The shaded gray region indicates the rms spread (see the text for details).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj509959f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadFigure/figure/apj509959f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj509959f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj509959f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj509959f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj509959f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Discrepancy in the position angle determined from SIE model fitting, P.A., and that measured from light distribution, P.A.<sub>*</sub>, as a function of <em>q</em><sub>SIE</sub> for 40 S4TM grade-A lenses. The shaded gray region indicates the rms spread (see the text for details).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj509959f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadFigure/figure/apj509959f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj509959f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj509959f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apj509959s3-2\" name=\"apj509959s3-2\">3.2.\u00a0Lens Modeling: Grade-C Lenses</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In comparison with \"grade-A\" lenses, the systems we refer to as \"grade-C lenses\" (i.e., without counterimages) are less informative about the mass structure of the foreground galaxy (in addition to being less visually striking). However, while these grade-C lenses do not provide accurate lens mass measurements, they do provide accurate lens-mass <em>probability distributions</em>. Furthermore, as will be seen later, grade-C systems are relatively less massive, and, hence, the lens-mass probability distributions that they provide are an essential element of our program to extend strong-lensing science to lower lens masses. Indeed, the inclusion of such single-image systems to a lens ensemble analysis makes the selection function less sharply dependent on lens-galaxy mass as compared to the grade-A lens sample alone.</p><p>Nevertheless, in most previous GL studies, grade-C lens systems have been ignored because of the difficulty or impossibility of obtaining definite lens mass models. (A significant counterexample is the use of lensing \"flexion\" to constrain mass models in systems that allow this technique: Goldberg &amp; Leonard <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib55\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib55\">2007</a>.) This difficulty is particularly pronounced within pure imaging surveys for lenses\u00a0because background galaxies with no clear multiple imaging can easily be confused with satellite galaxies of the foreground lens. For the case of the S4TM program, however, we have a strong prior for the identification of singly imaged background galaxies due to the original spectroscopic detection of a second redshift along the line of sight.</p><p>In this work, we explicitly incorporate grade-C lenses into our analysis, by first determining their posterior probability density functions (PDFs) of the Einstein\u00a0radius\u00a0and subsequently incorporating these PDFs into our ensemble analysis of the scaling relations of the lens population via a hierarchical Bayesian method. In order to obtain the posterior PDFs of <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub>, we utilize the <span class=\"small-caps\">MultiNest</span> package (Feroz &amp; Hobson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib45\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib45\">2008</a>; Feroz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib46\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib46\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib47\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib47\">2013</a>; Buchner et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib22\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib22\">2014</a>), which is a Bayesian inference tool designed to efficiently compute the evidence of models in high dimensions through nested sampling. For highly complex multimodal distributions with pronounced degeneracies, such as the grade-C lens modelings in our case, it has been demonstrated that <span class=\"small-caps\">MultiNest</span> significantly outperforms traditional Markov Chain Monte Carlo techniques. We assume uniform priors with appropriate bounds for all the lens and source parameters. In particular, we require the total mass profiles of grade-C lenses to be not flatter than the light distributions by explicitly setting <em>q</em><sub>SIE</sub> \u2265 <em>q</em><sub>*</sub>. This special requirement stems from the fact that the fitting code can converge to unphysical lens models with very small axis ratios and small lensing strength for grade-C systems. As shown previously by Koopmans et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib75\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib75\">2006</a>), Bolton et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib15\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib15\">2008b</a>), and Barnab\u00e8 et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib8\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib8\">2011</a>) and confirmed again by the grade-A subsample in the S4TM survey, the hypothesis of a mass quadrupole following the light quadrupole is generally valid, and the requirement of mass being not flatter than light is well-motivated. Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f11\">A1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f12\">A2</a> in the <xref summary=\" in the Appendix show the foreground\">Appendix</xref> show the foreground-subtracted images and posterior PDFs of <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> for S4TM and SLACS grade-C lenses, respectively.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj509959s3-3\" name=\"apj509959s3-3\">3.3.\u00a0Combined Sample</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We also run <span class=\"small-caps\">MultiNest</span> on grade-A lenses and find their posterior PDFs of <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> can be well described by extremely narrow Gaussian functions with means consistent with the best-fit <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> values from MPFIT. Therefore we approximate the posterior PDFs of <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> for grade-A lenses as delta functions. We then combine both posterior PDFs to perform unbiased analyses of the mass structure of ETGs across a wider range of galaxy masses. In the following sections, we combine these measurements with stellar velocity dispersions and broadband photometry to constrain the mass\u2013density profile and dark-matter fraction of ETGs as a function of galaxy mass.</p><p>Combining the S4TM survey and the SLACS survey generates a data set including 98 (40+58) grade-A and 33 (18+15) grade-C ETG lens systems. The mean redshift for the foreground lenses is <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn5.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {{z}_{L}}\\rangle =0.18$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {{z}_{L}}\\rangle =0.18</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn6.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {{z}_{S}}\\rangle =0.58$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {{z}_{S}}\\rangle =0.58</script></span></span> for the background sources. The distributions and median values of the stellar masses (derived from the <em>HST</em> F814W photometry assuming a Chabrier IMF and the fiducial stellar-population model of Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj509959s5\">5</a> below) for various subsamples are also plotted in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f4\">4</a>. Note that the S4TM lenses are generally less massive compared to the SLACS lenses, and grade-C lenses are generally less massive than grade-A lenses. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f5\">5</a> shows the distribution of the Einstein radius,\u00a0<em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub>, and stellar mass for all the 131 lenses. For grade-A lenses, only the measurements of <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> are plotted, as the uncertainties have been shown to be on the level of 0.5%, much smaller than the symbol size. For grade-C lenses, the most likely <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> values and the 1<em>\u03c3</em> uncertainties inferred from the posterior PDFs of <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> are displayed.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj509959f4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj509959f4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Distributions of the stellar mass,\u00a0<span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn7.gif\" alt=\"$M_{*}^{{\\rm Chab}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">M_{*}^{{\\rm Chab}}</script></span></span>, for the whole grade-A+C sample (black), SLACS grade-A subsample (blue), S4TM grade-A subsample (green), SLACS grade-C subsample (chocolate), and S4TM grade-C subsample (red) and the median values for each subsample. The stellar mass is derived from the <em>HST</em> I-band photometry by the SPS analysis, assuming a Chabrier IMF and other parameters (please refer to Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj509959s5\">5</a> for details).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj509959f4\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadFigure/figure/apj509959f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj509959f4\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj509959f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj509959f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj509959f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0Distribution of the predicted Einstein radii,\u00a0<em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub>, and the stellar mass,\u00a0<span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn8.gif\" alt=\"$M_{*}^{{\\rm Chab}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">M_{*}^{{\\rm Chab}}</script></span></span>, for all the modeled lenses. Open squares represent grade-A lenses, with 40 from the S4TM survey (green) and 58 from the SLACS survey (blue). We do not show uncertainties for grade-A lenses, as they have been quantified to be on the level of 0.5% (Figure <a href=\"#apj509959f1\">1</a>), much smaller than the symbol size. Grade-C lenses are filled circles with error bars indicating the 1<em>\u03c3</em> uncertainties inferred from the posterior PDFs.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj509959f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadFigure/figure/apj509959f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj509959f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj509959f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj509959s4\" name=\"apj509959s4\">4.\u00a0MASS-STRUCTURE ANALYSIS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In this section, we combine the posterior PDFs of <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> for both grade-A and grade-C lenses with lens-galaxy stellar velocity dispersions measured from SDSS spectroscopy in order to constrain the mass\u2013density profile of ETG lenses as a function of lens-galaxy mass. The inclusion of new S4TM systems and grade-C lenses allows us to explore a broader range of galaxy masses than previous studies.</p><h3 id=\"apj509959s4-1\" name=\"apj509959s4-1\">4.1.\u00a0Velocity-dispersion Proxy</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>To investigate the degree of central concentration of ETG mass profiles, we employ an observational proxy defined by the ratio of stellar velocity dispersion to the SIE model velocity dispersion,\u00a0<em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub>, defined in relation to the observable parameter,\u00a0<em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub>, through Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apj509959eqn1\">1</a>). It was suggested by Kochanek (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib70\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib70\">1994</a>) that this ratio, later denoted as <em>f</em><sub>SIE</sub> = <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub>/<em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub>, should be approximately unity for isothermal mass models, greater than unity for models more centrally concentrated than isothermal, and less than unity for models less centrally concentrated. Successive studies have confirmed this hypothesis and showed that <em>f</em><sub>SIE</sub> can be used as an empirical indicator of the mass\u2013density slope (Kochanek et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib71\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib71\">2000</a>; Treu &amp; Koopmans <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib119\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib119\">2002</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib120\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib120\">2004</a>; Koopmans et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib75\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib75\">2006</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib118\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib118\">2006</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib117\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib117\">2009</a>; Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib15\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib15\">2008b</a>; Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib6\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib6\">2010a</a>). We take this approach in the current work in order to investigate physical trends and their significance in the simplest possible manner\u00a0and defer a more detailed and self-consistent joint analysis of GL and stellar kinematics to future papers.</p><p>As mentioned above, the posterior PDFs of <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub> are converted directly from the posterior PDFs of <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub>. The other ingredient for our present analysis is the stellar velocity dispersion,\u00a0<em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub>, which is the standard deviation of velocities of stars within a galaxy. This quantity is determined spectroscopically by measuring the broadening of the galaxy spectrum due to the luminosity-weighted superposition of Doppler-shifted absorption lines from individual stars. Instead of adopting the preexisting SDSS stellar velocity-dispersion values calculated using a set of stellar spectra templates derived from the ELODIE stellar spectrum library (Prugniel &amp; Soubiran <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib98\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib98\">2001</a>) with wavelength coverage 4100\u22126800 \u00c5, we generate a new set of stellar templates from the Indo\u2013US library (Valdes et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib121\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib121\">2004</a>), patched and extended by selected synthetic spectra in the POLLUX database (Palacios et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib96\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib96\">2010</a>), covering the full wavelength range of observed spectra. More details about this procedure can be found in Bolton et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib16\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib16\">2012a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib17\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib17\">2012b</a>). The resulting velocity dispersions,\u00a0<em>\u03c3</em><sub>SDSS</sub>, are then corrected to values within one half of the half-light radius,\u00a0<em>R</em><sub>half</sub>/2 (a quantity that is comparable to the lens-galaxy Einstein radius as shown in Bolton et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib13\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib13\">2008a</a>) and confirmed again by our lens sample), following a compromise prescription between Jorgensen et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib66\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib66\">1995</a>), Mehlert et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib83\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib83\">2003</a>), and Cappellari et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib23\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib23\">2006</a>), as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj509959eqn3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959eqn3.gif\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{{\\sigma }_{*}}={{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SDSS}}}\\times {{\\left( \\frac{1.5^{\\prime\\prime} }{{{{\\rm R}}_{{\\rm half}}}/2} \\right)}^{0.05}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where 1.5'' is the angular radius of the SDSS fiber. The half-light\u00a0radius,\u00a0<em>R</em><sub>half</sub>, is derived from a core-S\u00e9rsic fit (Graham et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib58\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib58\">2003</a>) to the surface brightness distribution of each lens galaxy, as explained in Brownstein et al. (2015, in preparation).</p></div><h3 id=\"apj509959s4-2\" name=\"apj509959s4-2\">4.2.\u00a0Hierarchical Bayesian Analysis</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Our primary interest here is in the physical scaling relation between <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub> and <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub> that encodes the variation of the mass\u2013density profile within the ETG lens population. In the formalism of statistics, we treat this as a <em>conditional</em> PDF of <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub>, given <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub>.</p><p>We parameterize the mean scaling relation between <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn9.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn10.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}</script></span></span> as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj509959eqn4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959eqn4.gif\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\left( {{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}-2.35 \\right)=a\\times \\left( {{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}-2.35 \\right)+b,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>with a slope <em>a</em> and intercept <em>b</em>. The constant shift of 2.35 is introduced to reduce the correlation between <em>a</em> and <em>b</em>. Additionally, the value itself is chosen to match the average value of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn11.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}</script></span></span>. To constrain the parameters of this relation, we use the hierarchical Bayesian method (see, e.g., Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib17\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib17\">2012b</a>; Shu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib103\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib103\">2012</a>; Brewer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib18\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib18\">2013</a>; Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib105\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib105\">2013</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib106\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib106\">2014</a>). This method makes full use of all the observed information, deconvolves the observational uncertainties, and offers unbiased estimations of population \"hyperparameters\" that we are interested in. The hierarchical Bayesian approach also allows for straightforward inclusion of the posterior PDFs of <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> in the analysis.</p><p>Here we describe all the key ingredients in the hierarchical Bayesian analysis (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj509959t1\">1</a> for details). <em>x</em> and\u00a0<em>y</em> represent the two physical parameters,\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn13.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}},{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}},{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}</script></span></span>, and <em>I</em><sub><em>i</em></sub> and <em>y</em><sub><em>i</em></sub> are the corresponding observed data for the <em>i</em>th lens galaxy. The three hyperparameters (compacted as <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn14.gif\" alt=\"${\\boldsymbol{ \\theta }} $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\boldsymbol{ \\theta }}</script></span></span>) we want to determine are (1) <em>a</em>: the slope of the\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn15.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}</script></span></span>-<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn16.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}</script></span></span> relation; (2) <em>b</em>: the intercept; and (3) <em>\u03b4</em>: the intrinsic scatter in <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn17.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}</script></span></span>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apj509959t1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nKey Ingredients for the Hierarchical Bayesian Analysis\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj509959t1\" data-toolbar-img=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/suppdata/apj509959t1_lr.gif\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 1\"><colgroup><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col></colgroup><thead>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">Symbol</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">Definition</th>\n</tr>\n</thead><tbody>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">\n<em>x</em>\n</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">Parameter 1: <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img }} $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\boldsymbol{ \\theta }}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">\n<span
