rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">Grade A + C</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">0.64 \u00b1 0.06</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u22120.005 \u00b1 0.005</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p>\n<strong>Note.</strong> The best-estimated values of the two hyperparameters a and b derived from the corresponding marginal PDFs for the grade-A sub sample (second row) and grade-A+C sample (third row). By including the grade-C lenses, the slope becomes significantly shallower.</p>\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/suppdata/apj509959t2_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/803/2/71\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/suppdata/apj509959t2_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>We see that for the grade-A lens subsample, the overall mass\u2013density profile is essentially consistent with a mass-independent model at about 1.1<em>\u03c3</em> (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn46.gif\" alt=\"$P\\left( a\\lt 1.0 \\right)=85.49\\%$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">P\\left( a\\lt 1.0 \\right)=85.49\\%</script></span></span>), as found in previous SLACS studies (e.g., Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib15\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib15\">2008b</a>; Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib6\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib6\">2010a</a>). Grade-C lenses alone cannot provide meaningful constraints on the hyperparameters, given such broad posterior PDF distribution. However, the combination of grade-A and grade-C systems in the analysis significantly shifts the grade-A results and provides evidence for a mass-dependent density profile at about 6<em>\u03c3</em>. The sense of this trend is for lower-mass (i.e., lower velocity dispersion) ETGs to have steeper mass\u2013density profiles and for higher-mass ETGs to have shallower profiles.</p><p>Note that our current results for grade-A systems are consistent with previous SLACS results of a velocity-dispersion-independent density profile. Similarly, the parameters from our current analysis of grade-A+C systems are substantially consistent with the results of our current grade-A-only analysis. Hence our detection of a velocity-dispersion-dependent density profile in this work is fundamentally due to the inclusion of new data for new lenses covering a wider baseline in mass\u00a0and is not an artifact of the new methods we have applied in the analysis of these data.</p><p>Consulting the empirical relation between the mass\u2013density profile slope,\u00a0<em>\u03b3</em>', and the observable <em>f</em><sub>SIE</sub> from Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib6\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib6\">2010a</a>), we find that a value of <em>b</em> = \u22120.0338 corresponds on average to an isothermal profile (<em>\u03b3</em>' = 2). Considering the best-fit value for <em>b</em> for the grade-A subsample, we find that it is somewhat inconsistent with the\u00a0isothermal value at about 2.3<em>\u03c3</em>. Including grade-C systems as well, the best <em>b</em> value is more strongly inconsistent with the isothermal value at &gt;5<em>\u03c3</em>. In both cases, the offset is in the sense of having a slightly superisothermal profile at the central lensing velocity\u00a0dispersion of the samples, consistent with previous SLACS findings (Koopmans et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib76\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib76\">2009</a>).</p></div><h3 id=\"apj509959s4-3\" name=\"apj509959s4-3\">4.3.\u00a0Systematic Effects on Mass Structure</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>As previously mentioned, several issues in the lens-modeling procedure have not been fully addressed yet, including the environmental contributions, discrepancy between <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> and <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>, and the intrinsic nonisothermality of the lens galaxies. In this section, we investigate the individual effects of the above issues on our mass-structure results.</p><h4 id=\"apj509959s4-3-1\" name=\"apj509959s4-3-1\">4.3.1.\u00a0Environmental Effects</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Here we discuss the environmental effects on our mass-structure estimation caused by neglecting possible extra contributions from the environment, i.e., MSD and external shear. Following the recipe in Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib117\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib117\">2009</a>), we write the \"true\" total convergence as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj509959eqn15\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959eqn15.gif\" alt=\"Equation (15)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\kappa \\left( x,y \\right)=\\frac{\\sqrt{q}}{2}\\frac{R_{{\\rm SIE}}^{0}}{\\sqrt{{{x}^{2}}+{{q}^{2}}{{y}^{2}}}}+{{\\kappa }_{{\\rm ext}}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t15\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where the first term on the right-hand side is the contribution from the SIE lens, and the second term is used to quantify the contribution from external convergence (i.e., mass sheet). <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn47.gif\" alt=\"$R_{{\\rm SIE}}^{0}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">R_{{\\rm SIE}}^{0}</script></span></span> is the \"true\" Einstein radius for the lens galaxy, assuming an SIE model.</p><p>The total projected mass within an ellipse of axis ratio,\u00a0<em>q,</em> and semiminor axis,\u00a0<em>R</em>', is</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj509959eqn16\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959eqn16.gif\" alt=\"Equation (16)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}\\begin{array}{rcl} {{M}_{{\\rm total}}} & = & {{{\\rm \\ \\Sigma\\ }}_{{\\rm crit}}}\\iint \\kappa \\left( x,y \\right)dxdy \\\\ {} & = & \\pi {{{\\rm \\ \\Sigma\\ }}_{{\\rm crit}}}\\left( R_{{\\rm SIE}}^{0}{{R}^{{\\prime} }}+{{R}^{{\\prime} }}^{2}{{\\kappa }_{{\\rm ext}}} \\right), \\\\ \\end{array}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t16\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn48.gif\" alt=\"$R^{\\prime} =R/\\sqrt{q}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">R^{\\prime} =R/\\sqrt{q}</script></span></span> (geometric mean of the semimajor and semiminor axes), and \u03a3<sub>crit</sub> is the critical density. Note that here, for first-order approximation, a\u00a0uniform external convergence,\u00a0<em>\u03ba</em><sub>ext</sub>, is assumed. In the \"intermediate axis\" convention we have been working with, the Einstein radius,\u00a0<em>R</em><sub>SIE,\u00a0</sub>we would infer by neglecting the external convergence is given by</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj509959eqn17\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959eqn17.gif\" alt=\"Equation (17)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&R_{{\\rm SIE}}^{2}=\\frac{{{M}_{{\\rm total}}}}{\\pi {{{\\rm \\ \\Sigma\\ }}_{{\\rm crit}}}}=R_{{\\rm SIE}}^{0}{{R}_{{\\rm SIE}}}+R_{{\\rm SIE}}^{2}{{\\kappa }_{{\\rm ext}}}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t17\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>Therefore, the \"true\" Einstein radius,\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn49.gif\" alt=\"$R_{{\\rm SIE}}^{0}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">R_{{\\rm SIE}}^{0}</script></span></span>, differs from the value inferred from our SIE model,\u00a0<em>R</em><sub>SIE</sub>, by a factor of (1 \u2212 <em>\u03ba</em><sub>ext</sub>)</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apj509959eqn18\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959eqn18.gif\" alt=\"Equation (18)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&R_{{\\rm SIE}}^{0}={{R}_{{\\rm SIE}}}\\left( 1-{{\\kappa }_{{\\rm ext}}} \\right).\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t18\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><p>From the scaling law between the total projected mass and radius, Treu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib117\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib117\">2009</a>) estimated that the external convergence,\u00a0<em>\u03ba</em><sub>ext</sub>, for the SLACS lenses is no more than a few percent, and no significant bias was detected. Therefore we expect the effect of neglecting external convergence in our analysis is subdominant to measurement errors. Additionally, as suggested by Bolton et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib17\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib17\">2012b</a>), the external convergences are fluctuations about the mean densities\u00a0and only introduce random noise rather than bias, which will be smoothed out within our analysis.</p><p>The external shear,\u00a0<em>\u03b3</em>, that we choose to dismiss turns out to be also a minor effect. In order to quantify it, we fit an SIE+external shear model with fixed axis ratio and position angle to the S4TM grade-A lenses and compared the resulted Einstein radii to values obtained by an SIE model. The overall fractional discrepancy is on the level of 2%, which suggests that the effect is again subdominant. To explore any degeneracy between external shear and the axis ratio, we generate a set of mock lensed images from an SIE+external shear model with uniformly distributed axis ratios for the SIE profile. The lens galaxy and source are placed at typical lens and source redshifts, respectively. Typical noise level is also added to the mock images. The amplitude of the external shear is set to the mean shear found by Holder &amp; Schechter (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib62\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib62\">2003</a>) at 0.11 (also consistent with results in Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib128\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib128\">2011</a>), and the position angle of the external shear is marginalized over. We then fit the mock images using an SIE model\u00a0and compare the\u00a0Einstein radii so obtained to the fiducial value. The results are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f8\">8</a>. It is clear that we are able to recover the Einstein radii within an accuracy of about 4% for the entire axis-ratio range. More importantly, we do not observe any significant correlation between the discrepancy in the Einstein radius and the axis ratio.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj509959f8\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj509959f8\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f8_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 8.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 8.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f8_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 8.</strong>\u00a0Fractional discrepancy of the Einstein radius (<em>top</em>) and ratio of axis ratio (<em>bottom</em>) as a function of the \"true\" axis ratio when fitting mock images generated from an SIE+external shear model by an SIE model (please refer to the text for details).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj509959f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadFigure/figure/apj509959f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj509959f8\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj509959f8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h4 id=\"apj509959s4-3-2\" name=\"apj509959s4-3-2\">4.3.2.\u00a0\n<em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> Convention versus <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> Convention</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>As previously mentioned, the <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> convention for an elliptical case is generally not identical to the <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> convention for a circular case. The conversion between <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> and <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> has been derived in Huterer et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib65\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib65\">2005</a>) as a function of ellipticity (or axis ratio,\u00a0<em>q,</em> in our model). By definition, the deviation from <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> converges to 0 as <em>q</em> approaches 1. Here we use all the 98 grade-A lenses to explore the sensitivity of our final results to the different conventions.</p><p>We first convert <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> obtained by the SIE modeling to <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> following Huterer et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib65\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib65\">2005</a>)\u00a0and then incorporate <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub> predicted by <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> into the Bayesian analysis. Given the facts that the mean axis ratio of the grade-A sample is 0.789 and there is no significant correlation between <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub> and <em>q</em>, converting <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> to <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> will only introduce an almost constant offset of \u22120.002, on average, in <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn50.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}</script></span></span>, which consequently gets propagated into an offset in <em>b,\u00a0</em>but leaves the slope,\u00a0<em>a,</em> untouched. This expectation has been verified, as we find that the posterior PDF of <em>a</em> and <em>b</em> is almost identical, except for a tiny positive shift (~0.001) in the marginal PDF of <em>b</em>.</p><p>We think the grade-A subsample is representative of the entire sample in terms of the axis-ratio distribution. Therefore, we conclude that the choice of different conventions in the analysis is only a minor effect on the final mass-structure results.</p></div><h4 id=\"apj509959s4-3-3\" name=\"apj509959s4-3-3\">4.3.3.\u00a0Intrinsic Nonisothermality</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>It has been suggested in this paper that the intrinsic mass profile is a varying function of galaxy mass, being more superisothermal in lower-mass galaxies. This deviation from isothermality confirms recent findings by Sonnenfeld et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib105\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib105\">2013</a>), Dutton &amp; Treu (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib37\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib37\">2014</a>), and Sonnenfeld et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib106\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib106\">2014</a>). The effect of neglecting the intrinsic nonisothermality in the SIE modeling needs to be investigated. In order to do that, we fit 40 S4TM grade-A lenses by a singular power-law ellipsoid (SPLE) model for five discrete logarithmic mass\u2013density slopes,\u00a0<em>\u03b3</em>', from 1.5 to 2.5, and compare the lensing strength so obtained,\u00a0<em>b</em><sub>SPLE</sub>, which we think is a better approximation for the lensing velocity dispersion, to <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub>. We find that the majority of them suggests a correlation between the deviation from the \"true\" value defined as <em>b</em><sub>SIE</sub> \u2212 <em>b</em><sub>SPLE</sub> and the logarithmic slope,\u00a0<em>\u03b3</em>'. Following the mass-structure trend observed in this paper, it indicates that we tend to overestimate <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub> in low-mass galaxies (<em>\u03b3</em>' &gt; 2) and underestimate <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub> in high-mass galaxies (<em>\u03b3</em>' &lt; 2). That would in turn yield an even more pronounced mass-structure trend than we have seen. We defer further quantitative studies to future papers. Nevertheless, we confirm that neglecting the intrinsic nonisothermality cannot alleviate the detected mass dependence of the total mass\u2013density profile in the central region.</p></div></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj509959s5\" name=\"apj509959s5\">5.\u00a0STELLAR MASSES AND INITIAL MASS FUNCTION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In addition to allowing measurements of the shape of the lens mass\u2013density profile, strong-lensing data can be combined with photometry and stellar-population diagnostics to constrain the dark-matter fraction and/or stellar IMF in the lens (e.g., Ferreras et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib44\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib44\">2010</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib116\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib116\">2010</a>; Grillo &amp; Christensen <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib60\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib60\">2011</a>; Spiniello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib107\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib107\">2011</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib109\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib109\">2012</a>; Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib104\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib104\">2012</a>; Brewer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib19\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib19\">2014</a>). For this purpose, we estimate stellar masses of lens galaxies based on scaling of the SSP models to <em>HST</em> I-band photometry under a range of stellar-population assumptions\u00a0and adopting either a Chabrier or Salpeter IMF. This simplicity is motivated by uniformity, since although multiband <em>HST</em> photometry is available for many of the SLACS lenses, all the new S4TM systems currently have I-band data alone. High-resolution <em>HST</em> imaging is essential to masking the contribution from lensed background sources when performing lens-galaxy photometric modeling (Brownstein et al. 2015, in preparation). For this reason, we disregard multiband SDSS photometric magnitudes in our analysis.</p><p>To translate photometry into stellar masses, we make use of SSP models obtained with the Flexible Stellar Population Synthesis (FSPS: Conroy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib31\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib31\">2009</a>; Conroy &amp; Gunn <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib30\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib30\">2010</a>). Without colors or narrow-band indices, we must necessarily make assumptions about population parameters, such as the formation time after the Big Bang,\u00a0<em>t</em><sub>form</sub>, metallicity, and dust in the FSPS code. Since all the lens galaxies are by selection ETGs at relatively low redshifts, we adopt a reference model with typical values of <em>t</em><sub>form</sub> = 4 Gyr, solar metallicity, and no dust (Gallazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib48\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib48\">2006</a>; Carson &amp; Nichol <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib25\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib25\">2010</a>). We cross-check our stellar mass estimations with the values obtained by Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib5\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib5\">2009</a>) from multiband <em>HST</em> photometric data and a Bayesian stellar-population analysis approach for 52 confirmed SLACS lenses in common and find good agreement, with no bias observed. To quantify the systematic uncertainty of the simple treatment, we also consider lower- and upper-bound models. Our lower-bound model is dust-free and metal-poor (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn51.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}Z/{{Z}_{}}=-0.30$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}Z/{{Z}_{}}=-0.30</script></span></span>), while our upper-bound model is dusty and metal-rich (the optical depth for the dust attenuation,\u00a0<em>\u03c4</em> = 0.95, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn52.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}Z/{{Z}_{}}=0.20$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}Z/{{Z}_{}}=0.20</script></span></span>).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj509959fn3\">11</a></sup>\n The level of the resulting systematic variation in \u00a0the estimated stellar mass is around 0.5 dex. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f9\">9</a> shows the stellar masses of the 130 lenses from both the S4TM survey (filled symbols) and the SLACS survey (open symbols) for the reference model as a function of the stellar velocity dispersion. In accordance with the well-known Faber\u2013Jackson relation (Faber &amp; Jackson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib41\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib41\">1976</a>), galaxies with higher velocity dispersions also have higher stellar masses, on average.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj509959f9\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj509959f9\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f9_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 9.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 9.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f9_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 9.</strong>\u00a0Stellar masses,\u00a0<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>, of the 130 lenses inferred from the SPS analysis for the two IMFs, as a function of the stellar velocity dispersion. Filled symbols are for the S4TM lenses, and open symbols are for the SLACS lenses. Grade-C lenses are shown by stars, and grade-A lenses are circles. Colors represent the redshifts of the lens galaxies. Gray error bars represent the systematic variations (see the text for details on how to determine the error bars). The correlation of scatter with redshift is primarily driven by Malmquist bias in the parent samples.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj509959f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadFigure/figure/apj509959f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj509959f9\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj509959f9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We next examine the relationships between the projected dark-matter fraction within one half of the half-light\u00a0radius,\u00a0<em>f</em><sub>dm</sub>, defined as <em>f</em><sub>dm</sub> \u2261 1 \u2212 <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>(<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn53.gif\" alt=\"$\\lt {{R}_{{\\rm half}}}/2$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\lt {{R}_{{\\rm half}}}/2</script></span></span>)/<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn154.gif\" alt=\"${{M}_{{{R}_{{\\rm half}}}/2}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{M}_{{{R}_{{\\rm half}}}/2}}</script></span></span>, and the stellar mass, the stellar velocity dispersion, and the half-light\u00a0radius, respectively. The half-light\u00a0radius,\u00a0<em>R</em><sub>half</sub>, is determined by the core-S\u00e9rsic fit as the radius within which the enclosed light is one half of the total profile light. The stellar mass within one half of the half-light\u00a0radius,\u00a0<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>(&lt;<em>R</em><sub>half</sub>/2), is interpolated according to the underlying core-S\u00e9rsic profile under the assumption of a constant stellar-mass-to-light ratio for each galaxy. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f10\">10</a>, symbols correspond to predictions by the model of <em>t</em><sub>form</sub> = 4 Gyr, solar metallicity, and no dust for the two IMFs, respectively. In particular, stars represent grade-C lenses, and circles represent grade-A lenses. Filled symbols are S4TM lenses, while open symbols are SLACS lenses. The colors encode the redshifts of lens galaxies. Gray error bars show the systematic variations in <em>f</em><sub>dm</sub> throughout the parameter space, as explained above. From Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f10\">10</a>, we see general trends toward higher projected dark-matter fractions in galaxies with higher masses, larger velocity dispersions, and bigger sizes, consistent with detections by Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib6\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib6\">2010a</a>), using confirmed SLACS lenses. In all cases, the intrinsic scatter in <em>f</em><sub>dm</sub> is appreciable. More specifically, panel (a) shows a strong correlation between <em>f</em><sub>dm</sub> and the stellar mass,\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn54.gif\" alt=\"$M_{*}^{{\\rm Chabrier}}\\left( \\lt {{R}_{{\\rm half}}}/2 \\right)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">M_{*}^{{\\rm Chabrier}}\\left( \\lt {{R}_{{\\rm half}}}/2 \\right)</script></span></span>, for either IMF, which we will thoroughly discuss in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj509959s6-1\">6.1</a>. The same behavior can also be seen between panels (b), which display\u00a0the relations between <em>f</em><sub>dm</sub> and the stellar velocity dispersion <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub>. Panel (c) suggests that bigger lens galaxies (larger <em>R</em><sub>half</sub> values) have larger matter-to-luminosity ratios in their central regions. These can be explained by the idea that the surface density of dark matter varies less slowly (as compared to the luminous matter) as the galaxy becomes more massive. It is also in line with previously detected correlations between <em>\u03b3</em>' and the stellar mass density (Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib105\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib105\">2013</a>; Dutton &amp; Treu <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib37\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib37\">2014</a>). Of particular note, by implying a <em>negative</em> dark-matter fraction, the data strongly disfavor a Salpeter IMF for a large fraction of the lenses, especially for those with stellar velocity dispersions,\u00a0<em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub>, smaller than approximately 180 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>. This confirms a similar finding from Brewer et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib20\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib20\">2012</a>) based on a much smaller number of spiral lens galaxies.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj509959f10\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj509959f10\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f10_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 10.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 10.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f10_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 10.</strong>\u00a0Relations between the dark-matter fraction,\u00a0<em>f</em><sub>dm</sub>, and <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn56.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}M_{*}^{{\\rm Chabrier}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}M_{*}^{{\\rm Chabrier}}</script></span></span>, <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn57.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}</script></span></span>, and <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn58.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}\\;{{R}_{{\\rm half}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}\\;{{R}_{{\\rm half}}}</script></span></span>, respectively. Filled symbols represent S4TM lenses, while open symbols are for SLACS lenses. Grade-C lenses are shown by stars, and grade-A lenses are circles. Colors represent the redshifts of the lens galaxies. Gray error bars represent the systematic variations. Either a Chabrier or Salpeter IMF is considered.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj509959f10\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadFigure/figure/apj509959f10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj509959f10\" href=\"/0004-637X/803/2/71/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj509959f10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We have several avenues to improve the stellar-mass estimation. First of all, the significant scatter and systematic variation in <em>f</em><sub>dm</sub> and the IMF that we have not taken into account here will weaken any observed trends. A proper way to handle them is heavily required. On the lensing side, a simple SIE model for the total mass distribution, as considered in this work, although\u00a0a good approximation, is not able to distinguish the contributions from dark and baryonic matter. Further physical assumptions need to be made to break the mass\u2013density-profile-IMF degeneracy (Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib116\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib116\">2010</a>; Oguri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib95\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib95\">2014</a>). On the SPS side, age, metallicity, dust, and other parameters in the SPS models need to be better constrained. Additionally, it has been studied that the SPS technique highly relies on several IMF-sensitive spectral features such as Na<span class=\"small-caps\">i</span>, Ca<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>, FeH, TiO, and CaH1 (Conroy &amp; van Dokkum <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib32\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib32\">2012</a>; Spiniello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib108\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib108\">2014</a>). Improper interpretations in the SPS model\u00a0or lack of coverage of these features in the observed spectra can lead to significant systematics in constraining the IMF (Conroy &amp; van Dokkum <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib32\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib32\">2012</a>; Spiniello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib108\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib108\">2014</a>).</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj509959s6\" name=\"apj509959s6\">6.\u00a0DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In this paper, we report the discovery of 40 strong gravitational lenses with clear and definite multiple images (classified as \"grade-A\") and another 18 single-image lenses (classified as \"grade-C\") from the S4TM survey (<em>HST</em> Program ID 12210, Brownstein et al. (2015, in preparation)), which by design selects lens galaxies with lower masses and smaller Einstein radii, compared to the previous SLACS survey. Along with findings in the SLACS survey, we construct a statistically significant and more complete ensemble of over 100 gravitational lenses, including 98 grade-As and 33 grade-Cs. This combined sample probes ETGs with a mean lens redshift of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn59.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {{z}_{L}}\\rangle =0.18$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {{z}_{L}}\\rangle =0.18</script></span></span>. All lenses have been modeled individually, and measurements/posterior PDFs of the Einstein radii have been obtained for grade-A/C lens galaxies appropriately.</p><h3 id=\"apj509959s6-1\" name=\"apj509959s6-1\">6.1.\u00a0Discussion</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We have discovered clear evidence for the dependence of the total mass\u2013density profile on galaxy velocity dispersion, in the sense that less-massive (lower velocity\u00a0dispersion) lens galaxies have more centrally concentrated (superisothermal) profiles. We have obtained this result by performing a hierarchical Bayesian analysis of the relation between <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn60.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn61.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}</script></span></span> for the combined lens ensemble. The inclusion of grade-C lenses is essential to this discovery: the significance of the trend is about 6<em>\u03c3</em> when including grade-A and grade-C lenses together in the analysis, but only about 1<em>\u03c3</em> (i.e., consistent with no trend) when analyzing grade-A lenses alone. This can be attributed both to the fact that the grade-C lenses extend the mass baseline of the measurement to lower masses\u00a0and to the fact that excluding grade-C lenses will bias the sample toward\u00a0higher values of <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub> at fixed <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub>. Note that this trend is consistent with the overall trend seen going to even higher masses. In fact, in galaxy clusters, the slope is far from isothermal and closer to <em>\u03b3</em>' = 1 (Newman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib91\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib91\">2013a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib92\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib92\">2013b</a>).</p><p>A trend of mass\u2013density profile,\u00a0<em>\u03b3</em>', upon the surface stellar mass density,\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn62.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm \\ \\Sigma\\ }}_{*}}={{M}_{*}}/\\left( 2\\pi R_{{\\rm eff}}^{2} \\right)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm \\ \\Sigma\\ }}_{*}}={{M}_{*}}/\\left( 2\\pi R_{{\\rm eff}}^{2} \\right)</script></span></span>, has been found in Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib6\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib6\">2010a</a>), Sonnenfeld et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib105\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib105\">2013</a>), and Dutton &amp; Treu (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib37\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib37\">2014</a>), using either SLACS lenses or lenses from the Strong Lensing Legacy Survey (SL2S, Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib50\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib50\">2012</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib52\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib52\">2014</a>). In particular, they found that galaxies with denser stellar mass densities have steeper profiles. Since the stellar velocity dispersion can be approximated as <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn63.gif\" alt=\"$\\sigma _{*}^{2}\\propto {{M}_{*}}/{{R}_{{\\rm eff}}}\\propto {{{\\rm \\ \\Sigma\\ }}_{*}}{{R}_{{\\rm eff}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sigma _{*}^{2}\\propto {{M}_{*}}/{{R}_{{\\rm eff}}}\\propto {{{\\rm \\ \\Sigma\\ }}_{*}}{{R}_{{\\rm eff}}}</script></span></span>, the <em>\u03b3</em>'\u2013<em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub> relation and <em>\u03b3</em>'\u2013\u03a3<sub>*</sub> relation suggest that more massive galaxies are spatially less concentrated and have higher velocity dispersion at the present time. This can be understood in terms of the expectations from baryonic physics within DM halos. Dissipative gas-cooling processes lead to higher baryon densities in the center of DM halos\u00a0and also steepen the DM halo profile through the effect of adiabatic contraction (AC: e.g., Blumenthal et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib12\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib12\">1986</a>). Energetic feedback processes from SN and AGN tend to heat gas and counteract central condensation. These processes compete against each other in their effect on the mass distribution in DM halos. More importantly, the efficiencies of both cooling and feedback processes depend on galaxy mass/velocity dispersion. Our findings suggest that for less-massive galaxies, the impact of feedback is less significant, as compared to AC/cooling and hence leads to a more centrally concentrated halo. The importance of feedback increases as galaxies become more massive, resulting in shallower density profiles. Metallicity, environment, and other processes are also responsible for this competition. In order to determine whether the effect is regulated primarily by velocity dispersion or by stellar density, multiband data sufficient for detailed stellar-population analysis will be required for the full S4TM sample.</p><p>We have applied a simplified SPS analysis to the <em>HST</em> I-band photometric data to estimate the stellar masses of the lens galaxies, assuming either a Chabrier or a Salpeter IMF. Age, metallicity, and dust have been chosen to match the typical values for passively evolving ETGs at low redshifts. A clear correlation between the projected dark-matter fraction and the total mass is observed for both IMFs, consistent with previous findings (e.g., Tortora et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib113\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib113\">2009</a>; Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib6\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib6\">2010a</a>; Cappellari et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib24\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib24\">2012</a>; Conroy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib29\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib29\">2013</a>; Brewer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib19\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib19\">2014</a>). There are two possible interpretations of this result: as a true increase in dark-matter fraction with velocity dispersion, or as a trend in the stellar IMF with velocity dispersion. The first interpretation aligns with the overall expectation of decreased star-formation efficiency with increasing halo mass for halos above ~10<sup>8</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299\u00a0</sub>(e.g., Behroozi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib11\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib11\">2010</a>). This interpretation could also explain our observed trend in the dark-matter profile slope: for a fixed stellar profile shape, an increased fractional mass contribution from a more spatially extended DM halo will result in a shallower total mass\u2013density profile. Alternatively, a trend toward\u00a0a more bottom-heavy IMF in more massive galaxies can cause the apparent effect of an increased DM fraction when a single IMF is assumed across all masses (e.g., Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib116\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib116\">2010</a>; van Dokkum &amp; Conroy <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib123\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib123\">2010</a>; Strader et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib110\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib110\">2011</a>; Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib104\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib104\">2012</a>; Spiniello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib109\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib109\">2012</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib108\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib108\">2014</a>; Conroy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib29\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib29\">2013</a>; Ferreras et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib43\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib43\">2013</a>; Geha et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib53\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib53\">2013</a>; La Barbera et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib79\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib79\">2013</a>; Tortora et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib114\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib114\">2013</a>). Given the limited data set used in this paper, we are not able to distinguish between the two interpretations. Additional assumptions or data are necessary to break this degeneracy (e.g., Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib104\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib104\">2012</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib106\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib106\">2014</a>). However, the Salpeter IMF is in any event disfavored at the low-mass end, since it results in unphysical negative DM fractions.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj509959s6-2\" name=\"apj509959s6-2\">6.2.\u00a0Conclusion and Future Work</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>To conclude, in this paper,</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">We report the discovery of 40 new grade-A and 33 new grade-C ETG lenses from the S4TM and SLACS surveys. Besides the measurements for grade-A lenses, posterior PDFs of the Einstein radii for grade-C lenses are determined for the first time using the <span class=\"small-caps\">MultiNest</span> tool. Combining with 58 grade-A ETG lenses from the SLACS survey, we construct an ETG lens ensemble with wider mass coverage than previous strong-lens samples;</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Applying a hierarchical Bayesian method, which utilizes the posterior PDFs of the Einstein radii, we study the correlation between <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn64.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn65.gif\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}</script></span></span>\u00a0and find a less-than-unity slope of 0.64 \u00b1 0.06, which corresponds to a significant (\u22486<em>\u03c3</em>) dependence of total mass\u2013density profile on the lens mass in the sense that more massive ETGs possess shallower profiles (as quantified by the ratio of <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub> to <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIE</sub>, which serves as a proxy for the logarithmic mass\u2013density profile slope,\u00a0<em>\u03b3</em>'). We have shown that this trend is only significant when grade-C lenses are included (the slope is 0.93 \u00b1 0.06 for the grade-A-only subsample), which highlights the importance of grade-C lenses to enable\u00a0a wider coverage of lens masses;</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Stellar masses of lens galaxies are estimated based on their <em>HST</em> I-band photometry and SPS models, assuming either a Chabrier or Salpeter IMF. The resulting DM fractions within one half of the half-light radius <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> for each IMF model are found to be strongly correlated with the lens mass/velocity dispersion in the sense that more massive ETGs have larger DM fractions\u00a0or, alternatively, mass-dependent IMFs (or a combination of both effects). A Salpeter IMF is ruled out for ETGs with velocity dispersion smaller than 180 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, by implying negative <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>.</div></li></ul><p>\n</p><p>The analysis of our new S4TM lens sample in combination with other lens samples can be improved with spatially resolved long-slit or integral field spectroscopy in order to determine the 2D stellar kinematics of the lenses, which can in turn enable detailed lensing-plus-dynamical modeling to better constrain the mass\u2013density profile for individual galaxies and eventually break the MSD (Barnab\u00e8 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib9\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib9\">2009</a>; McKean et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib82\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib82\">2010</a>; van de Ven et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib122\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib122\">2010</a>; Barnab\u00e8 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib8\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib8\">2011</a>; Dutton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib36\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib36\">2011</a>; Newman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib90\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib90\">2011</a>; Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib104\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib104\">2012</a>). Multiband photometry or spectroscopy covering a wide wavelength range from near-ultraviolet (NUV) to near-infrared (NIR) for the lens galaxies would similarly yield better constraints on the age, metallicity, dust, and other parameters in the lens-galaxy SPS models. Finally, more sophisticated lens models with separate components for dark matter and stars would also improve upon our current single-component total-mass models (e.g., Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib51\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib51\">2007</a>; Dutton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib36\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib36\">2011</a>; Barnab\u00e8 et al. 2012; Vegetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib124\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib124\">2010</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib125\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib125\">2012</a>). With uniform data on a comprehensive lens sample from the SLACS, S4TM, and BELLS surveys covering a wide range in lens redshift (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959ieqn66.gif\" alt=\"$0.1\\lt z\\lt 0.7$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">0.1\\lt z\\lt 0.7</script></span></span>) and total enclosed mass (10<sup>10</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> &lt; <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> &lt; 10<sup>12</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>), we can fully explore the variation of the ETG mass structure across galaxy mass and cosmic time through a joint analysis of strong lensing, stellar dynamics, and stellar populations.</p></div></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The authors thank the anonymous referee for insightful comments and suggestions that substantially improved this paper. The support and resources from the Center for High Performance Computing at the University of Utah is gratefully acknowledged. T.T. acknowledges support from the Packard Foundation through a Packard Research Fellowship. R.G. acknowledges support for the Centre National des Etudes Spatiales. The work of L.A.M. was carried out at Jet Propulsion Laboratory, California Institute of Technology, under a contract with NASA. Support for Program 12210 was provided by NASA through a grant from the Space Telescope Science Institute, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc., under NASA Contract NAS 5-26555.</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj509959app1\" name=\"apj509959app1\">APPENDIX</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>HST F814W foreground-subtracted images of all 33 grade-C lenses in this work along with the posterior PDFs of the Einstein radius are shown in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f11\">A1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj509959f12\">A2</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj509959f11\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj509959f11\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/Full/apj509959f11a_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure A1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj509959bib31\" id=\"fnref-apj509959bib31\">2009</a>).</p></p></div></li></ul>\n\t\t</div>\n\t<!-- End Footnotes -->\t\n<!-- Start References List -->\n\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon exp-w-hash references\">\n                <div class=\"replica-h3 bdt-1 article-references__title\">\n                <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger article-references\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide&nbsp;references\"\n                   data-reveal-text=\"Show&nbsp;references\" aria-expanded=\"false\" id=\"references\">Show&nbsp;references</a>\n            </div>\n                <div class=\"reveal-content\" id=\"references-wrapper\">\n                    <div class=\"rss-feed__spinner-wrapper\"><span class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Please wait&hellip; <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">THE SLOAN LENS ACS SURVEY. All rights reserved.</span><br />\n    <!-- end Copyright -->\n<span itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Periodical\" itemid=\"periodical\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-breadcrumb-title article-toolbar-anchor\">\n\t<span itemprop=\"name\">\n\t\t<a href=\"/journal/0004-637X\" itemprop=\"url\">The Astrophysical Journal</a></span></span>,\n\t<span itemprop=\"isPartOf\" itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/PublicationVolume\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-breadcrumb-vol nowrap\">\n\t<link itemprop=\"isPartOf\" itemid=\"periodical\" itemscope/>\n\t<span itemprop=\"volumeNumber\"><a href=\"/volume/0004-637X/803\" itemprop=\"url\">Volume 803</a></span></span>,\n<span itemprop=\"issueNumber\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-breadcrumb-issue\"><a href=\"/issue/0004-637X/803/2\" itemprop=\"url\"><!--Issue Number Tile Start-->\nNumber 2<!--Issue Number Tile End--></a></span>\n<!--Article Breadcrumb Tile End-->\n<!-- Start Focus issue title -->\n\t<!-- End focus issue title -->\n\t<span style=\"display: block\"><b>Citation</b> Yiping Shu <em>et al</em> 2015 <em>ApJ</em> <b>803</b> 71</span>\n    </p>\n\n<div class=\"btn-multi-block mb-1\">\n\t\t<a href=\"/article/10.1088/0004-637X/803/2/71/pdf\" class=\"btn btn-large btn-primary content-download btn-full-w-mobile wd-jnl-art-pdf-button-main dupe-buttons\" title=\"THE SLOAN LENS ACS SURVEY. <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F803%2F2%2F71\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/803/2/71</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <P>We present observational results from a new <I>Hubble Space Telescope</I> (<I>HST</I>) Snapshot program to extend the methods of the Sloan Lens ACS (SLACS) Survey to lower lens-galaxy masses. We discover 40 new galaxy-scale strong lenses, which we supplement with 58 previously discovered SLACS lenses. In addition, we determine the posterior PDFs of the Einstein radius for 33 galaxies (18 new and 15 from legacy SLACS data) based on single lensed images. We find a less-than-unity slope of 0.64 \u00b1 0.06 for the <IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{*}}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/apj509959ieqn1.gif\"></IMG>\u2212<IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"${{{\\rm log} }_{10}}{{\\sigma }_{{\\rm SIE}}}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/803/2/71/apj509959ieqn2.gif\"></IMG> relation, which corresponds to a 6<I>\u03c3</I> evidence that the total mass\u2013density profile of early-type galaxies varies systematically in the sense of being shallower at higher lens-galaxy velocity dispersions. The trend is only significant when single-image systems are considered, highlighting the importance of including both \"lenses\" and \"nonlenses\" for an unbiased treatment of the lens population when extending to lower mass ranges. By scaling simple stellar-population models to the <I>HST</I> I-band data, we identify a strong trend of increasing dark-matter fraction at higher velocity dispersions, which can be alternatively interpreted as a trend in the stellar initial mass function (IMF) normalization. Consistent with previous findings and the suggestion of a nonuniversal IMF, we find that a Salpeter IMF is ruled out for galaxies with velocity dispersion less than 180 km s<SUP>\u22121</SUP>. Considered together, our mass-profile and dark-matter fraction trends with increasing galaxy mass could both be explained by an increasing relative contribution on kiloparsec scales from a dark-matter halo with a spatial profile more extended than that of the stellar component.</P></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a
